href="https://orcid.org/0009-0003-1037-6247" property="identifier" aria-label="ORCID identifier" target="_blank">https://orcid.org/0009-0003-1037-6247</a></span></span><span data-hidden-on="all">, <span property="author" typeof="Person" role="listitem"><a href="#con6"><span property="givenName">Rania-Cérine</span> <span property="familyName">Mazouzi</span></a></span></span><span data-hidden-on="all">, <span property="author" typeof="Person" role="listitem"><a href="#con7"><span property="givenName">Florian</span> <span property="familyName">Levet</span></a> <a class="orcid-id" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-4009-6225" property="identifier" aria-label="ORCID identifier" target="_blank">https://orcid.org/0000-0002-4009-6225</a></span></span><span data-hidden-on="all">, <span property="author" typeof="Person" role="listitem"><a href="#con8"><span property="givenName">Alexandre</span> <span property="familyName">Favereaux</span></a> <a property="author" typeof="Person" role="listitem"><a href="#con10"><span property="givenName">Olivier</span> <span property="familyName">Thoumine</span></a> <a class="orcid-id" data-id="article-nav-menubar-share" disabled="true" aria-disabled="true" aria-controls="core-collateral-share" title="Share" tabindex="-1" data-toggle="tooltip" data-placement="left"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-share"></i><span class="sr-only">Share</span></a></li></ul></nav></div></div><div id="abstracts" data-extent="frontmatter"><div class="core-container"><section id="executive-summary-abstract" property="abstract" typeof="Text" role="doc-abstract"><h2 property="name">Significance</h2><div role="paragraph">The formation of synapses, the communication nodes between neurons, is of utmost importance in brain development. Adhesion molecules including neuroligins (NLGNs) play important roles in this process by providing a linkage between pre- and postsynaptic compartments, and by recruiting functional complexes through signaling mechanisms. In the absence of reliable antibodies, investigating NLGN function has heavily relied on the use of primary neurons over- or underexpressing recombinant NLGNs, inducing potential artifacts. Instead of manipulating NLGN expression level, we generated and characterized a KI mouse model in which endogenously tagged NLGN1 can be detected with high affinity by biochemistry and microscopy, revealing insight into the organization of NLGN1 at both excitatory and inhibitory synapses.</div></section><section id="abstract" property="abstract" typeof="Text" role="doc-abstract"><h2 property="name">Abstract</h2><div role="paragraph">Neuroligins (NLGNs) are important cell adhesion molecules mediating trans-synaptic contacts between neurons. However, the high-yield biochemical isolation and visualization of endogenous NLGNs is hampered by the lack of efficient antibodies. Thus, to reveal their subcellular distribution, binding partners, and synaptic function, NLGNs were extensively manipulated using knock-down, knock-out, or overexpression approaches, leading to controversial results. As an alternative to the manipulation of NLGN expression level, we describe here the generation of a knock-in (KI) mouse strain in which native NLGN1 was N-terminally tagged with a small biotin acceptor peptide (bAP) that can be enzymatically biotinylated by the exogenous delivery of biotin ligase. After showing that KI mice exhibit normal behavior as well as similar synaptic number, ultrastructure, transmission properties, and protein expression levels when compared to wild type counterparts, we exploited the fact that biotinylated bAP-NLGN1 can be selectively isolated or visualized using high-affinity streptavidin conjugates. Using immunoblotting and immunofluorescence, we show that bAP-NLGN1 binds PSD-95 and gephyrin and populates both excitatory and inhibitory synapses, challenging the historical view that NLGN1 is exclusively localized at excitatory synapses. Using superresolution optical and electron microscopy, we further highlight that bAP-NLGN1 forms in the synaptic cleft a subset of nanodomains, which contain each a few NLGN1 dimers and whose number positively scales with the postsynapse size. Overall, our study not only provides an extensively characterized KI mouse model which will be available to the scientific community but also an unprecedented view of the nanoscale organization of endogenous NLGN1.</div></section>



        
        
   <div class="signup-alert-ad">
    <div class="d-flex justify-content-between align-items-center">
        <div>
            <h3 class="signup-alert-ad__title">Sign up for PNAS alerts.</h3>
            <p class="signup-alert-ad__content mt-2">Get alerts for new articles, or get an alert when an article is cited.</p>
        </div>
        <div> 
            <a href="/action/showPreferences?menuTab=Alerts" class="btn btn-primary mt-2 signup-alert-ad__action">Manage alerts</a>
        </div>
    </div>
    </div>
</div></div><section id="bodymatter" data-extent="bodymatter" property="articleBody" typeof="Text"><div class="core-container"><div data-type="flushleft" role="paragraph">The assembly of the trillions of synapses formed between neurons during brain development is controlled by many factors including cell adhesion molecules. Among them, neuroligins (NLGN1-4) play critical roles in synapse formation, and are the target of deleterious genetic mutations in autism spectrum disorders (<a href="#r1" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r1">1</a>, <a href="#r2" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r2">2</a>). NLGNs form constitutive dimers that interact extracellularly with neurexins (NRXNs) in an isoform- and splice variant-dependent manner (<a href="#r3" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r3">3</a>–<a href="#r5" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r5">5</a>), as well as with MDGAs which compete with trans-synaptic NRXN-NLGN adhesion (<a href="#r6" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r6">6</a>, <a href="#r7" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r7">7</a>). Intracellularly, NLGNs bind PDZ domain containing scaffolding proteins of excitatory synapses through their C-terminus (<a href="#r8" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r8">8</a>–<a href="#r11" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r11">11</a>), the inhibitory synaptic scaffolding molecule gephyrin through a proximal consensus motif (<a href="#r12" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r12">12</a>, <a href="#r13" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r13">13</a>), the actin-regulating WAVE complex through a WIRS motif located upstream the C-terminus (<a href="#r14" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r14">14</a>), and a still unidentified regulatory partner through a noncanonical motif located close to the transmembrane domain (<a href="#r15" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r15">15</a>). The synaptic function of NLGNs is also regulated by a number of phosphorylation sites recognized by various serine/threonine and tyrosine kinases (<a href="#r16" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r16">16</a>–<a href="#r21" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r21">21</a>).</div><div role="paragraph">Despite these advances, the high-yield biochemical isolation and superresolution imaging of endogenous NLGNs have been hampered by the lack of efficient antibodies to these proteins. Hence, alternative strategies were developed based on the overexpression or rescue of tagged NLGNs in cultured neurons to either isolate or visualize NLGNs. For instance, the expression of HRP-NLGN2 served to characterize the inhibitory synapse proteome (<a href="#r22" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r22">22</a>), while GFP or split-GFP tagged NLGNs were used to localize these proteins and study their dynamics at synapses (<a href="#r23" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r23">23</a>–<a href="#r27" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r27">27</a>). However, the introduction of relatively large tags can alter the function of a protein by modifying interactions with specific partners, and overexpression might bias its localization. Indeed, although endogenous NLGN1 is traditionally associated to excitatory synapses and NLGN2 to inhibitory synapses (<a href="#r28" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r28">28</a>, <a href="#r29" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r29">29</a>), the overexpression of either NLGN isoform increases the number of both excitatory and inhibitory synapses, associated to a gain of function in synaptic transmission (<a href="#r18" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r18">18</a>, <a href="#r27" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r27">27</a>, <a href="#r30" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r30">30</a>). Along this line, the synaptic cleft proteome obtained by overexpressed HRP-NLGN1 reveals both excitatory and inhibitory synaptic interactors (<a href="#r22" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r22">22</a>). Conversely, NLGN1 knock-down decreases both excitatory and inhibitory synaptic puncta (<a href="#r31" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r31">31</a>, <a href="#r32" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r32">32</a>), overall suggesting that NLGN1 might also be implicated at inhibitory synapses. A decade ago, based on the expression of NLGN1 tyrosine point mutants, we proposed that the presence of NLGN1 at excitatory versus inhibitory synapses might be controlled by the differential binding of NLGN1 to PSD-95 versus gephyrin, regulated by phosphorylation (<a href="#r13" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r13">13</a>, <a href="#r18" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r18">18</a>).</div><div role="paragraph">An alternative to overexpression and knock-down issues and to the use of large fusion proteins has been to rescue endogenous NLGN1 by recombinant NLGN1 carrying small tags such as the biotin acceptor peptide (bAP), followed by labeling of biotinylated NLGN1 with fluorescent streptavidin to study its membrane dynamics and nanoscale localization (<a href="#r6" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r6">6</a>, <a href="#r24" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r24">24</a>, <a href="#r25" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r25">25</a>). However, even when doing so, the knock-down of native proteins with shRNAs or cre-recombinase is often incomplete, and the protein under the control of exogenous promoters might still be slightly overexpressed. Furthermore, the rescue construct is only one of several NLGN splice variants (e.g., NLGN1 carrying both A and B splice sites), such that its specific expression may bias the balance between excitatory and inhibitory synapses (<a href="#r33" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r33">33</a>–<a href="#r36" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r36">36</a>). Therefore, there is a crucial need for efficient approaches to target native NLGNs. In this direction, an earlier study reported on a constitutive knock-in (KI) mouse strain in which endogenous NLGN1 was fused to an HA tag (<a href="#r37" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r37">37</a>), that was recently used to visualize NLGN1 at high resolution through expansion microscopy in relation to presynaptic NRXNs and neurotransmitter receptors (<a href="#r38" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r38">38</a>). Although powerful, this approach still suffers from the fact that all brain cells including neurons and astrocytes—which also express NLGNs (<a href="#r39" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r39">39</a>)—are labeled, making the subsequent analysis of individual synapses difficult, and that anti-HA antibodies remain of moderate affinity.</div><div role="paragraph">To circumvent these limitations, we describe here the generation of a KI mouse strain in which endogenous NLGN1 was fused to an N-terminal bAP tag that can be enzymatically biotinylated by the viral delivery or electroporation of endoplasmic reticulum-resident biotin ligase (BirA<sup>ER</sup>), allowing cell-specific detection with high-affinity streptavidin conjugates. We first compared KI and WT mice regarding behavior, brain anatomy, synaptic transmission properties, expression levels of synaptic proteins, as well as synapse number and ultrastructure. We then exploited the biotinylation strategy to show an efficient pull-down of bAP-NLGN1 by streptavidin beads, revealing its interaction with NLGN2, NLGN3, PSD-95, and gephyrin. Furthermore, labeling neurons with fluorescent streptavidin combined with the staining of PSD-95 or gephyrin demonstrates that bAP-NLGN1 localizes to both excitatory and inhibitory synapses. Finally, we reveal quantitative details about the nanoscale organization of bAP-NLGN1 at synapses using superresolution fluorescence imaging and electron microscopy.</div><section id="sec-1" data-type="results"><h2>Results</h2><section id="sec-1-1"><h3>Generation of a bAP-NLGN1 KI Mouse Strain.</h3><div role="paragraph">In order to modify endogenous NLGN1 for its subsequent biotinylation, we designed a KI mouse strain in which the <i>Nlgn1</i> gene in chromosome 3 was N-terminally flanked by a small linker and a DNA sequence coding for the 15 amino acid bAP tag (<a href="#r40" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r40">40</a>), inserted after the signal peptide. The generation of the bAP-NLGN1 KI mouse strain was made at the <i>Phenomin</i> mouse clinics in Strasbourg (France) (<a href="http://www.phenomin.fr/">http://www.phenomin.fr/</a>). The strategy was based on the CRISPR/Cas9 method, using a guide RNA (gRNA) specific for <i>Nlgn1</i> (<a href="#fig01">Fig. 1<i>A</i></a>). This mouse strain was imported to our animal facility, backcrossed with C57BL/6 J mice, and raised to the homozygote genotype. To validate the bAP tag insertion, we performed genomic DNA sequencing of <i>Nlgn1</i> that displayed the correct sequence (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S1 <i>A</i> and <i>B</i></a>), as well as genotyping that showed the expected shifts in the PCR products from KI mice as compared to wild type (WT) mice of the same genetic background (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S1<i>C</i></a>).</div><div class="figure-wrap"><header><div class="label">Fig. 1.</div></header><figure id="fig01" class="graphic"><img src="/cms/10.1073/pnas.2411669122/asset/92999a46-9d6f-4fc4-9fce-b7a681652095/assets/images/large/pnas.2411669122fig01.jpg" height="1232" width="1469" aria-labelledby="fig01" loading="lazy" /><figcaption>Behavioral characterization of KI mice. (<i>A</i>) Design of the bAP-NLGN1 KI mouse strain. (<i>B</i>) Body weight of WT and KI mice over time. (<i>C</i>–<i>E</i>) Behavioral assessment of WT and KI mice at different ages. Grouped analysis on WT and KI mice was made for each parameter (general aspect, sensory properties, locomotion, and anxiety). Data were compared by a two-way ANOVA, showing no significant (n.s.) difference between genotypes. (<i>F</i>) Schematics of the elevated plus maze test. (<i>G</i>) Representative heat maps showing the cumulated positions of representative WT and KI mice exploring their environment for 10 min. (<i>H</i>) Time spent in closed-arms. (<i>I</i>) Total distance traveled in apparatus. All values were obtained from 9 WT and 10 KI mice. Data are presented as mean ± SEM and were compared by Mann–Whitney tests (n.s.).</figcaption></figure></div></section><section id="sec-1-2"><h3>Behavioral Characterization of KI Mice.</h3><div role="paragraph">To assess whether the modification of endogenous NLGN1 by insertion of the bAP tag caused any alteration in mouse behavior or physiology, we compared equivalent groups of WT and KI mice bred in the same conditions. First, there was no difference in weight between WT and KI mice across a 6-mo period (<a href="#fig01">Fig. 1<i>B</i></a>). We then used the SHIRPA protocol (<a href="#r41" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r41">41</a>) to assess the behavioral phenotype of WT and KI mice at ages of 1 to 6 mo, using a wide range of parameters (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S2</a>). Grouped analysis for general aspect, locomotion, sensory properties, and anxiety from 6 mo old KI mice did not reveal any difference compared to WT (<a href="#fig01">Fig. 1 <i>C</i> and <i>E</i></a>). However, a tendency for a decrease in general anxiety parameters was observed in 3-mo old KI mice compared to WT (<a href="#fig01">Fig. 1<i>D</i></a>). To specifically monitor anxious behavior, this scoring was complemented by an elevated plus maze test (<a href="#fig01">Fig. 1 <i>F</i> and <i>G</i></a>). No significant difference was observed in the time spent in the closed arms between the WT and KI groups (<a href="#fig01">Fig. 1<i>H</i></a>), nor in the total distance traveled by mice during the test (<a href="#fig01">Fig. 1<i>I</i></a>). Overall, this thorough analysis revealed no major behavioral anomaly in KI mice.</div></section><section id="sec-1-3"><h3>Normal Brain and Synapse Organization in KI Mice.</h3><div role="paragraph">To assess whether the brain of KI mice showed anatomical alterations when compared to WT counterparts, we performed immunohistochemical stainings of fixed brain sections from P14 and P30 animals, focusing on the cortex and hippocampus where NLGN1 is highly expressed (<a href="#r42" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r42">42</a>). We first stained cell nuclei with cresyl violet to visualize cell layers in the cortex (<a href="#fig02">Fig. 2 <i>A</i> and <i>B</i></a>). Analysis of the number of cell nuclei per surface area revealed no difference between sections from WT and KI mice (<a href="#fig02">Fig. 2<i>C</i></a>). In molecular layers, excitatory and inhibitory presynapses were immunostained for VGluT1 and VGAT (<a href="#fig02">Fig. 2 <i>D</i> and <i>E</i></a>), respectively. The analysis showed no difference in the intensity (<a href="#fig02">Fig. 2 <i>F</i> and <i>G</i></a>) or surface area (<a href="#fig02">Fig. 2 <i>H</i> and <i>I</i></a>) of the fluorescence signals between sections from WT and KI mice. Similar results were found in hippocampal sections (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S3</a>). To count individual synapses, we cultured primary hippocampal neurons from either WT or KI mice pups (P0) for 14 d in vitro (DIV), and immunostained excitatory synapses with antibodies to VGluT1 and PSD-95, and inhibitory synapses with antibodies to VGAT and gephyrin (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S4 <i>A</i> and <i>B</i></a>). No difference in the surface density, area, or signal intensity of either excitatory or inhibitory synaptic puncta was found between the two genotypes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S4 <i>C</i>–<i>E</i></a>). Finally, to assess whether synapse ultrastructure is affected in KI mice, we performed transmission electron microscopy (TEM) of ultrathin sections of cortical tissue from either WT or KI mice (<a href="#fig02">Fig. 2 <i>J</i> and <i>K</i></a>). The surface densities of excitatory and inhibitory synapses were similar in WT and KI mice, at both P14 and P30 (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S5</a>). Moreover, within synapses, the presynaptic surface area (<a href="#fig02">Fig. 2<i>L</i></a>), the numbers of neurotransmitter vesicles and mitochondria (<a href="#fig02">Fig. 2 <i>M</i> and <i>N</i></a>), and the PSD size (<a href="#fig02">Fig. 2<i>O</i></a>) were similar between the two genotypes. Therefore, there was no detectable alteration of brain anatomy, synapse number and type, or synapse ultrastructure in KI mice.</div><div class="figure-wrap"><header><div class="label">Fig. 2.</div></header><figure id="fig02" class="graphic"><img src="/cms/10.1073/pnas.2411669122/asset/65de700c-9308-464a-88ad-019e19f96931/assets/images/large/pnas.2411669122fig02.jpg" height="1888" width="1294" aria-labelledby="fig02" loading="lazy" /><figcaption>Brain development and synapse ultrastructure in KI mice. (<i>A</i>) Diagram of cortical slices ranging from Bregma −1.5 to −1.9 mm, used for immunohistochemistry. (<i>B</i>) Representative Nissl staining in sections from WT and KI mice. (<i>C</i>) Cell density in slices from either genotype (n.s.). (<i>D</i> and <i>E</i>) Immunohistochemical staining of VGLUT1 (red) and VGAT (magenta), respectively, performed on cortical slices from WT and KI mice. Fluorescence signal intensity (<i>F</i> and <i>G</i>) and surface area (<i>H</i> and <i>I</i>), of VGluT1 and VGAT staining, respectively, normalized to WT levels (in %). Data represent mean ± SEM of n = 8 hemispheres from 4 mice for each genotype. Statistical comparison was made by two-way ANOVA, showing no significant difference between WT and KI on all measurements. (<i>J</i> and <i>K</i>) Electron micrographs showing excitatory and inhibitory synapses from the cortex of WT and KI mice. Pre- and postsynaptic elements appear in yellow and blue, respectively, and the synaptic cleft is shown with arrowheads. (<i>L</i>–<i>N</i>) Surface area, number of neurotransmitter vesicles, and number of mitochondria in presynapses, respectively. (<i>O</i>) PSD size of excitatory synapses. Data represent mean ± SEM of 59 synapses from 2 WT mice, and 70 synapses from 2 KI mice (no significant difference between WT and KI for any of the parameters).</figcaption></figure></div></section><section id="sec-1-4"><h3>Normal Synaptic Transmission in KI Mice.</h3><div role="paragraph">To determine whether the bAP tagging of endogenous NLGN1 affected synaptic transmission, we prepared acute hippocampal slices from WT and KI mice and performed patch-clamp recordings of pyramidal CA1 neurons. We stimulated Schaffer's collaterals that form well-characterized synapses onto CA1 neurons (<a href="#r15" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r15">15</a>, <a href="#r18" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r18">18</a>). By changing the holding membrane potential in voltage-clamp, we sequentially recorded AMPA- and NMDA-receptor mediated EPSCs in response to increasing electrical stimuli (<a href="#fig03">Fig. 3<i>A</i></a>). There was no difference of evoked EPSCs (<a href="#fig03">Fig. 3 <i>B</i> and <i>C</i></a>), NMDA/AMPA ratio (<a href="#fig03">Fig. 3<i>D</i></a>), nor paired pulse ratio (PPR) (<a href="#fig03">Fig. 3 <i>E</i> and <i>F</i></a>) between neurons from WT or KI mice. We also measured spontaneous AMPA receptor-mediated EPSCs occurring in sweeps between stimulations, and found no difference in either the amplitude or frequency of those events between genotypes (<a href="#fig03">Fig. 3 <i>G</i>–<i>I</i></a>). Thus, excitatory synaptic transmission is not altered in KI mice, at least at the level of these canonical synapses.</div><div class="figure-wrap"><header><div class="label">Fig. 3.</div></header><figure id="fig03" class="graphic"><img src="/cms/10.1073/pnas.2411669122/asset/f3ff0282-0b8d-47c9-8101-35ec2ac1313b/assets/images/large/pnas.2411669122fig03.jpg" height="1364" width="1291" aria-labelledby="fig03" loading="lazy" /><figcaption>Synaptic transmission and protein expression levels in KI mice. (<i>A</i>) Representative voltage-clamp recordings performed on CA1 neurons in acute slices from WT or KI mice, upon stimulation of Schaffer’s collaterals. EPSCs were recorded sequentially in the same neuron at two holding potentials (−70 mV and +40 mV) to isolate AMPA- and NMDA-receptor mediated synaptic transmission, respectively. (<i>B</i> and <i>C</i>) Amplutides of NMDA-receptor and AMPA-receptor mediated EPSCs, respectively, versus electrical stimulation intensity. (<i>D</i>) Paired ratio between NMDA- and AMPA-receptor mediated EPSCs measured at a 40 V stimulation. (<i>E</i>) AMPA-receptor mediated EPSCs in response to two stimulations separated by 50 ms in slices from WT and KI mice. (<i>F</i>) Corresponding paired pulse ratio. (<i>G</i>) Traces of AMPA-receptor mediated spontaneous EPSCs in CA1 neurons. (<i>H</i> and <i>I</i>) Amplitude and frequency of those events in the two genotypes. Data are presented as mean ± SEM (n = 10 to 12 neurons from 3 independent slices) and analyzed by unpaired <i>t</i> test (n.s.). (<i>J</i>) Immunoblots of hippocampal cell lysates from WT and KI mice, β-actin being used as loading control. The molecular weight markers in kDa indicated on the <i>Left</i>. (<i>K</i>) Relative levels of various synaptic proteins, analyzed by semiquantitative immunoblotting (n = 4 to 5 independent cultures for both WT and KI conditions). Data represent mean ± SEM and were compared by individual Mann–Whitney tests for each protein (**<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01, all other differences being n.s.) (<i>L</i>) Western blot of proteins extracted from cortical tissue of WT or KI mice at different developmental ages, and probed with NLGN1 antibody. Total protein staining was used as a loading control. (<i>M</i>) Corresponding NLGN1 expression level at each age. Dots in the bars represent individual animals (n = 4 from 2 independent experiments). Data represent the mean value and were compared by multiple Mann–Whitney tests (*<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05).</figcaption></figure></div></section><section id="sec-1-5"><h3>Biochemical Analysis of Synaptic Protein Expression in KI Mice.</h3><div role="paragraph">To determine the levels of postsynaptic proteins classically associated with NLGN1, we processed lysates of hippocampal cultures from WT and KI mice at DIV14 by SDS-PAGE, followed by immunoblotting with antibodies to NLGN1, NLGN2, NLGN3, PSD-95, gephyrin, MDGA1, the NMDA receptor subunit GluN1, and the AMPA receptor subunits GluA1 and GluA2 (<a href="#fig03">Fig. 3<i>J</i></a>). When normalized to β-actin bands on the same blots, all proteins showed comparable levels in cultures from WT and KI mice, except for bAP-NLGN1 which exhibited a 26 % decrease in level compared to native NLGN1 from WT mice, and NLGN3 which showed a 17 % increase between KI and WT levels (<a href="#fig03">Fig. 3<i>K</i></a>). The band representing bAP-NLGN1 appeared slightly above that of endogenous NLGN1 in cultures from WT mice (123.2 ± 2.0 vs 121.7 ± 2.1 kDa, n = 7 experiments), as expected from the minor molecular weight addition brought by the bAP tag and linker (20 aa = 2 kDa). Moreover, the bAP tag (GLNDIFEAQKIEWHE) contains an asparagine that could be N-linked glycosylated, as reported for N303 in the NLGN1 splice site B (<a href="#r34" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r34">34</a>), but the small molecular weight shift observed here does not argue for such a posttranslational modification. To assess whether the decrease in global bAP-NLGN1 level was also present in the membrane fraction, we performed biotinylation of surface proteins, followed by streptavidin pull-down and immunoblotting of NLGN1. When normalized to total protein amount, the surface level of NLGN1 was also decreased by 29 ± 2% in KI compared to WT cultures (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S6</a>). Finally, to check whether the lower bAP-NLGN1 level was linked to the culture preparation, we collected fresh cortical tissue from WT and KI mice at various ages (P0, P7, P14, and P28 after birth), solubilized proteins, and performed Western blots against NLGN1 (<a href="#fig03">Fig. 3<i>L</i></a>). The NLGN1 level was again 25 ± 4% lower in KI mice compared to WT mice at P0 (corresponding to the difference seen in hippocampal cultures), but gradually reached the same level as in WT mice as animals grew older (<a href="#fig03">Fig. 3<i>M</i></a>).</div><div role="paragraph">Having shown in this first part of the study that the replacement of native NLGN1 by bAP-NLGN1 does not significantly alter mouse behavior, brain and synapse development, synaptic transmission, and the expression levels of common synaptic markers except for a modest reduction in NLGN1 levels, we used a targeted biotinylation strategy combined with high-affinity streptavidin conjugates to selectively isolate and visualize bAP-NLGN1, as a proxy to endogenous NLGN1.</div></section><section id="sec-1-6"><h3>Specific Streptavidin Pull-Down of bAP-NLGN1 and Protein Partners.</h3><div role="paragraph">To biotinylate bAP-NLGN1, we infected primary hippocampal neurons from KI mice at DIV3-5 with adeno-associated viruses (AAV1) coding for the ER resident enzyme biotin ligase (BirA<sup>ER</sup>-IRES-GFP, called BirA+) (<a href="#r41" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r41">41</a>), which covalently attaches exogenously supplied biotin to a central lysine residue in the bAP tag (<a href="#r40" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r40">40</a>) (<a href="#fig04">Fig. 4<i>A</i></a>). As controls, we used cultures from KI mice infected with AAV1 coding for GFP alone (IRES-GFP, called BirA-), and neurons from WT mice infected with BirA+ (<a href="#fig04">Fig. 4<i>B</i></a>). We lysed neurons at DIV14, isolated biotinylated proteins from the lysates using streptavidin coated beads, and performed SDS-PAGE and immunoblots to GFP, HA, or NLGN1. As expected, GFP was detected in the starting material of all samples, while the HA tag on BirA<sup>ER</sup> was detected only in the BirA+ condition (<a href="#fig04">Fig. 4<i>C</i></a>). NLGN1 was pulled down very efficiently by streptavidin beads from bAP-NLGN1 neurons infected by BirA+, but not in the three other conditions, showing a highly selective isolation of biotinylated NLGN1 (<a href="#fig04">Fig. 4<i>D</i></a>). Indeed, the NLGN1 level present in the flow through was only 22% of its initial level in the starting material (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S7</a>). We then used this property to pull down proteins associated to NLGN1. We detected large amounts of endogenous NLGN3 associated to bAP-NLGN1 (<a href="#fig04">Fig. 4 <i>E</i> and <i>F</i></a>), most likely revealing the formation of NLGN1-NLGN3 heterodimers, as already reported (<a href="#r4" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r4">4</a>, <a href="#r43" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r43">43</a>). bAP-NLGN1 also pulled significant amounts of PSD-95 (<a href="#fig04">Fig. 4<i>E</i></a>), as expected from its well characterized C-terminal interaction with PSD-95 at both biochemical and immunocytochemical levels (<a href="#r8" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r8">8</a>, <a href="#r10" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r10">10</a>, <a href="#r31" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r31">31</a>). More surprisingly, gephyrin was also consistently found in bAP-NLGN1 pull-downs (<a href="#fig04">Fig. 4<i>E</i></a>), confirming that gephyrin can interact with endogenous NLGN1 in neurons as initially predicted from in vitro assays (<a href="#r12" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r12">12</a>, <a href="#r13" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r13">13</a>). A sizeable fraction of NLGN2 was also detected in the bAP-NLGN1 pull-down, indicating either a direct extracellular interaction or an indirect binding via gephyrin (<a href="#fig04">Fig. 4 <i>E</i> and <i>F</i></a>).</div><div class="figure-wrap"><header><div class="label">Fig. 4.</div></header><figure id="fig04" class="graphic"><img src="/cms/10.1073/pnas.2411669122/asset/0f181337-d8b7-46c8-bfa7-49211f839145/assets/images/large/pnas.2411669122fig04.jpg" height="1256" width="988" aria-labelledby="fig04" loading="lazy" /><figcaption>Isolation of biotinylated bAP-NLGN1 and binding partners by streptavidin pull-down. (<i>A</i>) Schematics of NLGN1 containing the 15 aa N-terminal bAP tag that can be enzymatically biotinylated by BirA<sup>ER</sup>, then labeled with streptavidin conjugates. (<i>B</i>) Hippocampal cultures from WT or KI mice were infected at DIV3-5 with BirA<sup>ER</sup>-HA-IRES-GFP AAV1 (BirA+), or control IRES-GFP AAV1 (BirA-). (<i>C</i> and <i>D</i>) Cultures were lysed at DIV14, and protein extracts were either loaded as starting material or precipitated with streptavidin beads. Separated proteins were identified by Western blot with antibodies to HA, GFP, or NLGN1 (molecular weight markers in kDa indicated on the <i>Left</i>). (<i>E</i>) Cultures from KI mice infected with BirA+ or BirA- AAV1 were lysed at DIV14 and protein extracts were immunoblotted for NLGN2, NLGN3, PSD-95, and gephyrin. (<i>F</i>) Band intensity for the 3 NLGN isoforms in the BirA+ condition, normalized to the signal in the BirA- condition (value of 1). Dots in the bars represent individual cultures.</figcaption></figure></div></section><section id="sec-1-7"><h3>Specific Labeling of bAP-NLGN1 with Fluorescent Streptavidin.</h3><div role="paragraph">To examine the surface localization of bAP-NLGN1 by optical microscopy, we infected dissociated hippocampal neurons from WT or KI mice at DIV3-5 with BirA+ or BirA- AAV1s, and live stained the cultures at DIV14 with Alexa-647-conjugated streptavidin (SA-AF647) (<a href="#fig05">Fig. 5 <i>A</i> and <i>B</i></a>). Strikingly, only neurons from KI mice infected with BirA+ showed significant SA-AF647 staining, while neurons infected with BirA- showed minimal staining, as low as neurons from WT mice infected with BirA+. Similar levels of SA-AF647 staining were observed in neurons electroporated with the BirA<sup>ER</sup> plasmid and in neurons infected by BirA+ AAV1, suggesting that BirA<sup>ER</sup> maximally biotinylates bAP-NLGN1. Moreover, electroporation of neurons from KI mice with an shRNA to NLGN1 (<a href="#r25" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r25">25</a>, <a href="#r31" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r31">31</a>) resulted in a 63 ± 3% decrease in SA-AF647 staining compared to a control shRNA (<a href="#fig05">Fig. 5 <i>C</i> and <i>D</i></a>). Together, these data demonstrate that streptavidin specifically labels native bAP-NLGN1 in its biotinylated form. In addition, we performed coculture experiments (<a href="#r44" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r44">44</a>) by plating COS-7 cells expressing GFP-NRXN1β or GFP-GPI as a negative control, onto DIV7 neurons from KI mice previously infected with BirA+ AAV1. After 48 h to allow neurites to contact COS-7 cells, we labeled neurons with SA-AF647. Dendrites formed many SA-AF647 positive contacts with COS-7 cells expressing GFP-NRXN1β, but not GFP-GPI (<a href="#fig05">Fig. 5 <i>E</i> and <i>F</i></a>), demonstrating that biotinylated endogenous bAP-NLGN1 binds recombinant NRXN1β.</div><div class="figure-wrap"><header><div class="label">Fig. 5.</div></header><figure id="fig05" class="graphic"><img src="/cms/10.1073/pnas.2411669122/asset/73b282b7-9c27-4a25-b52b-0a029de6f499/assets/images/large/pnas.2411669122fig05.jpg" height="971" width="1292" aria-labelledby="fig05" loading="lazy" /><figcaption>Detection of biotinylated bAP-NLGN1 with fluorescent streptavidin. (<i>A</i>) Dissociated hippocampal neurons from WT or KI mice infected with BirA+ or BirA- AAV1 at DIV3-5 (GFP reporter in green) were live labeled at DIV14 with SA-AF647 (magenta) and visualized by epifluorescence microscopy. (<i>B</i>) SA-AF647 intensity on GFP-expressing neurons in each condition. Dots show &gt; 20 cells per condition from 3 independent experiments. Data represent mean ± SEM and were compared by a two-way ANOVA followed by Sidak’s multiple comparison test (****<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001). (<i>C</i>) Hippocampal neurons from KI mice electroporated at DIV0 with BirA<sup>ER</sup> and shRNAs to either p53 (shCTRL) or NLGN1 (shNLGN1), both containing a GFP reporter (green), were labeled at DIV14 with SA-AF647 (magenta). (<i>D</i>) SA-AF647 signal on GFP-expressing neurons for each condition. Dots show 52 cells per condition from 2 independent experiments, and data represent mean ± SEM compared by a Mann–Whitney test (****<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001). (<i>E</i>) COS-7 cells electroporated with GFP-GPI or GFP-NRXN1β were added to DIV8 hippocampal cultures from KI mice previously infected with BirA+ AAV1, and after 48 h cocultures were labeled with SA-AF647 (magenta). (<i>F</i>) Fractional area occupied by SA-AF647 clusters on COS-7 cells, multiplied by the SA-AF647 intensity ratio between COS-7 cells and dendrites, in the two conditions. Dots show 22 to 38 cells per condition from 3 separate experiments, and data represent mean ± SEM compared by a Mann–Whitney test (****<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001).</figcaption></figure></div></section><section id="sec-1-8"><h3>bAP-NLGN1 Is Localized at Both Excitatory and Inhibitory Synapses.</h3><div role="paragraph">Interestingly, streptavidin-labeled bAP-NLGN1 was enriched in puncta localized in dendritic spines and corresponding to excitatory postsynaptic densities, but was also found in more elongated structures throughout the dendritic shaft, seemingly corresponding to inhibitory postsynapses. To confirm this dual localization of bAP-NLGN1, we subsequently immunostained the cultures for synapsin1, VGluT1 or VGAT, and found that bAP-NLGN1 was indeed present in front of both excitatory and inhibitory presynaptic markers, but also displayed a 23% extrasynaptic population (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S8</a>). Then, to examine the postsynaptic distribution of bAP-NLGN1, we coelectroporated dissociated neurons from KI mice with BirA<sup>ER</sup> and intrabodies to either PSD-95 (<a href="#r45" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r45">45</a>) or gephyrin (<a href="#r46" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r46">46</a>), fused respectively to mRuby2 and GFP (<a href="#fig06">Fig. 6<i>A</i></a>), and quantified the fraction of bAP-NLGN1 colocalizing with those markers (<a href="#fig06">Fig. 6 <i>B</i>–<i>D</i></a>). In parallel, we checked by counterimmunostaining that these intrabodies specifically labeled endogenous PSD-95 and gephyrin, respectively (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S9</a>). Strikingly, bAP-NLGN1 accumulated as strongly at PSD-95 and gephyrin puncta (enrichment in postsynapse versus shaft around 3), and the proportion of PSD-95 or gephyrin puncta that contained bAP-NLGN1 were respectively 70 ± 2% and 54 ± 3% (<a href="#fig06">Fig. 6 <i>E</i> and <i>F</i></a>), showing that more than half of inhibitory postsynapses actually recruit NLGN1. The proportion of bAP-NLGN1 within PSD-95 and gephyrin puncta was 57% and 15%, respectively (<a href="#fig06">Fig. 6<i>D</i></a>), in relation to the fact that there are around two times more excitatory synapses than inhibitory synapses in those cultures (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Figs. S4 <i>A</i>–<i>C</i> and S8<i>E</i></a>). There was also a 22 % fraction of extrasynaptic bAP-NLGN1 clusters (<a href="#fig06">Fig. 6 <i>C</i> and <i>D</i></a>), significantly smaller than synaptic bAP-NLGN1 clusters (<a href="#fig06">Fig. 6<i>G</i></a>), and a 6 % fraction of bAP-NLGN1 that colocalized with both PSD-95 and gephyrin (<a href="#fig06">Fig. 6 <i>C</i> and <i>D</i></a>), likely corresponding to doubly innervated dendritic spines (<a href="#r47" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r47">47</a>).</div><div class="figure-wrap"><header><div class="label">Fig. 6.</div></header><figure id="fig06" class="graphic"><img src="/cms/10.1073/pnas.2411669122/asset/9d7c11b6-fe1d-4b50-85ad-217815999aa3/assets/images/large/pnas.2411669122fig06.jpg" height="2192" width="1291" aria-labelledby="fig06" loading="lazy" /><figcaption>Subcellular localization of biotinylated bAP-NLGN1 at excitatory and inhibitory synapses. (<i>A</i>) Dissociated hippocampal neurons from KI mice were coelectroporated at DIV0 with BirA<sup>ER</sup> and intrabodies to PSD-95 and gephyrin (Xph20-mRuby2 and GPHN.FingR-GFP, respectively). (<i>B</i>) At DIV14, neurons were live-labeled with SA-AF647 and visualized by epifluorescence microscopy. Image of a dendritic arbor showing bAP-NLGN1 labeled with SA-AF647 (magenta), PSD-95 (cyan), and gephyrin (green) positive synapses. A specific segment (rectangle) is zoomed on the <i>Right</i>, and insets highlight individual synapses, with SA-AF647 clusters overlapping with PSD-95 (yellow arrows), or gephyrin (orange arrows) puncta. (<i>C</i>) Line scans across synapses showing NLGN1 clusters either in extrasynaptic locations (ExtraSyn), overlapping with PSD-95 (PSD-95+), with gephyrin (GPHN+), or with both PSD-95 and gephyrin (PSD-95+/GPHN+). (<i>D</i>) Distribution of bAP-NLGN1 clusters in the different compartments. (<i>E</i> and <i>F</i>) Enrichment of bAP-NLGN1 at PSD-95 or gephyrin puncta, and fraction of these puncta overlapping with bAP-NLGN1, respectively. Dots in the bars represent n = 39 cells for each condition, from 2 independent experiments. Data represent mean ± SEM and were compared by a Mann–Whitney test (*<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05, ***<i>P</i> &lt; 0.001). (<i>G</i>) Surface area of bAP-NLGN1 clusters as a function of their localization (n = 436 to 2224 clusters in 39 cells, from 2 independent experiments). Data were compared by a Kruskal–Wallis test followed by Dunn’s multiple comparison test (****<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001).</figcaption></figure></div></section><section id="sec-1-9"><h3>bAP-NLGN1 Forms Nanodomains in both Excitatory and Inhibitory Postsynapses.</h3><div role="paragraph">We further examined the nanoscale localization of biotinylated bAP-NLGN1 stained with SA-AF647 using direct STochastic Optical Reconstruction Microscopy (dSTORM) (<a href="#r6" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r6">6</a>, <a href="#r24" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r24">24</a>, <a href="#r25" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r25">25</a>), in relation to postsynapses labeled with the specific intrabodies described above (<a href="#fig07">Fig. 7 <i>A</i> and <i>B</i></a>). Superresolved maps integrating all single molecule localizations revealed a diffuse distribution of bAP-NLGN1 in the dendritic shaft, as well as a specific accumulation at excitatory and inhibitory postsynapses, with a similar synaptic versus shaft enrichment in single molecule localizations around 4 (<a href="#fig07">Fig. 7<i>C</i></a>). As a control, we quantified the surface density of bAP-NLGN1 localizations in random extrasynaptic areas of size comparable to postsynaptic puncta, and normalized these values with respect to the shaft. As expected, the average bAP-NLGN1 enrichment in these extrasynaptic clusters lies close to 1 (<a href="#fig07">Fig. 7<i>C</i></a>). We then analyzed the subsynaptic distribution of bAP-NLGN1 using dedicated software (<a href="#fig07">Fig. 7</a> D, E). bAP-NLGN1 was found to form several small domains (between 0 and 5, average around 1) in both excitatory and inhibitory postsynapses (<a href="#fig07">Fig. 7<i>F</i></a>), whose size ranged between 30-100 nm (<a href="#fig07">Fig. 7<i>G</i></a>), and which covered rather uniformly the area of PSD-95 and gephyrin puncta (<a href="#fig07">Fig. 7<i>H</i></a>). Those nanodomains were highly enriched, i.e. reaching a density of single molecule detections 80-fold larger than that in the shaft (<a href="#fig07">Fig. 7 <i>I</i> and <i>K</i></a>). Strikingly, the number of bAP-NLGN1 nanodomains (<a href="#fig07">Fig. 7 <i>J</i> and <i>L</i></a>), but not their size (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S10 <i>C</i> and <i>D</i></a>), positively correlated with the projected area of the corresponding PSD or gephyrin puncta. By normalizing the integrated number of single molecule localizations in postsynapses by the corresponding number of single SA-AF647 molecules isolated on a coverslip and imaged under the same conditions (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S10 <i>E</i>–<i>J</i></a>), we computed that 10.0 ± 0.6 SA-AF647 molecules were bound to biotinylated bAP-NLGN1 in excitatory postsynapses (mean ± SEM from 1139 PSD-95 positive puncta), and 11.3 ± 0.9 SA-AF647 molecules in inhibitory postsynapses (from 631 gephyrin positive puncta). The numbers of SA-AF647 within bAP-NLGN1 nanodomains located inside PSD-95 or gephyrin puncta were 5.3 ± 0.3 and 4.7 ± 0.3, respectively. Increasing the concentration of SA-AF647 did not improve the specific fluorescence signal on neurons expressing BirA<sup>ER</sup>, suggesting that biotinylated bAP-NLGN1 binding sites are saturated by streptavidin.</div><div class="figure-wrap"><header><div class="label">Fig. 7.</div></header><figure id="fig07" class="graphic"><img src="/cms/10.1073/pnas.2411669122/asset/6daf0da7-717c-44da-bc34-44b92567d152/assets/images/large/pnas.2411669122fig07.jpg" height="2120" width="1643" aria-labelledby="fig07" loading="lazy" /><figcaption>Superresolved localization of biotinylated bAP-NLGN1. (<i>A</i>–<i>L</i>) Dissociated hippocampal neurons from KI mice were electroporated at DIV0 with BirA<sup>ER</sup> and either Xph20-GFP or GPHN.FingR-GFP. dSTORM experiments were performed at DIV14 on fixed cultures, after live-labeling neurons with SA-AF647. (<i>A</i> and <i>B</i>) Images of dendritic segments showing PSD-95 or gephyrin positive postsynapses (green) and the superresolved map of single bAP-NLGN1 molecule detections (gold). On the <i>Right</i>, individual examples of excitatory and inhibitory postsynapses. (<i>C</i>) Enrichment of bAP-NLGN1 localization density in PSD-95 or gephyrin puncta, or control extrasynaptic puncta, normalized to the average value on the dendritic shaft. Dots in the bars represent individual cells. Data represent mean ± SEM and were compared by a Kruskal–Wallis test followed by Dunn’s multiple comparison tests (****<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001). (<i>D</i> and <i>E</i>) Images of bAP-NLGN1 localizations constructed with SR-Tesseler, showing several nanodomains (gold) within PSD-95 and gephyrin puncta, respectively (white contours). The corresponding surface area of postsynaptic puncta is indicated. (<i>F</i>–<i>H</i>) Frequency distribution of the number, length, and distance from puncta center respectively, of bAP-NLGN1 nanodomains within PSD-95 and gephyrin puncta. (<i>I</i> and <i>K</i>) Average surface density of bAP-NLGN1 detections throughout the dendritic shaft, in postsynapses or in synaptic nanodomains, for either PSD-95 or gephyrin puncta. Dots represent individual cells, for each type of compartment. Data were compared by a Kruskal–Wallis test followed by Dunn’s multiple comparison test (****<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001). (<i>J</i> and <i>L</i>) Correlation between the bAP-NLGN1 nanodomain number and the PSD-95 or gephyrin surface area, respectively. Dots represent individual synaptic puncta. r, Pearson’s correlation coefficient; black line, linear regression with 95% CI (gray). All data related to PSD-95+ puncta were obtained from 4 independent experiments (1390 domains/1065 synaptic puncta/43 cells). Data related to GPHN+ puncta were obtained from 3 independent experiments (984 domains/631 synaptic puncta/36 cells). (<i>M</i>) Hippocampal neurons from KI mice electroporated with BirA<sup>ER</sup> were cocultured with neurons from WT mice electroporated with EGFP + mEos3.2-NRXN1β (green), resulting in the formation of trans-synaptic contacts between axonal NRXN1 and dendritic NLGN1. At DIV14, cultures were live labeled with SA-AF647 (magenta), then fixed and processed for sequential dSTORM and PALM. (<i>N</i>) Hippocampal neurons from KI mice infected with BirA+ AAV1 were labeled at DIV14 with SA-AF647 (magenta), then fixed and immunostained for RIM1/2 using a secondary antibody conjugated to AF532 (cyan), and processed for 2-color dSTORM. (<i>O</i> and <i>P</i>) Histograms of the nearest distance between the centroid of bAP-NLGN1 nanodomains and the outline of either presynaptic mEos3.2-NRXN1β or RIM1/2 densities, respectively. Each dataset was obtained from 2 independent experiments. The number of bAP-NLGN1 synaptic puncta analyzed is indicated on each graph.</figcaption></figure></div></section><section id="sec-1-10"><h3>bAP-NLGN1 Nanodomains Align with Presynaptic GFP-NRXN1β and RIM1/2.</h3><div role="paragraph">To check that bAP-NLGN1 interacts with presynaptic NRXNs, we cocultured hippocampal neurons from KI mice electroporated with BirA<sup>ER</sup>, with hippocampal neurons from WT mice electroporated with EGFP and recombinant NRXN1β N-terminally fused to the photoconvertible protein mEos3.2. At DIV14, cultures were live labeled with SA-AF647, then fixed and processed for sequential dSTORM and PhotoActivated Localization Microscopy (PALM) acquisitions. bAP-NLGN1 nanodomains in dendrites were in close contact to mEos3.2-NRXN1β clusters in axons (average nearest neighbor distance = 150 nm), revealing the formation of trans-synaptic adhesions (<a href="#fig07">Fig. 7 <i>M</i> and <i>O</i></a>). We also performed 2-color dSTORM experiments on neurons from KI mice infected with BirA+ AAV1 and labeled at DIV14 with SA-AF647, then fixed and immunostained for the active zone protein RIM1/2, a hallmark of trans-synaptic nanocolumns (<a href="#r48" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r48">48</a>). bAP-NLGN1 was localized in front of RIM1/2 clusters (<a href="#fig07">Fig. 7 <i>N</i> and <i>P</i></a>), with an average nearest neighbor distance of 102 nm, indicating that native NLGN1 is part of nanocolumns, as previously shown for recombinant HA-NLGN1 (<a href="#r49" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r49">49</a>).</div></section><section id="sec-1-11"><h3>bAP-NLGN1 Populates both Excitatory and Inhibitory Synapses in Organotypic Slices.</h3><div role="paragraph">To assess the synaptic localization of bAP-NLGN1 in more complex tissue than primary 2D cultures, we turned to organotypic hippocampal slices (<a href="#r18" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r18">18</a>), in which neuronal architecture and synaptic connectivity is well preserved, as in acute slices (<a href="#r50" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r50">50</a>). Slices from either WT or KI mice were infected with BirA+ AAV9 at DIV3, incubated live with SA-AF647 at DIV14, then fixed and observed in confocal microscopy (<a href="#fig08">Fig. 8 <i>A</i>–<i>D</i></a>). There was a specific SA-AF647 signal on slices from KI—but not WT—mice, preferentially localized in the CA3 region, which might reflect a better sensitivity to the AAV9 infection (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials" target="_blank"><i>SI Appendix</i>, Fig. S11</a>). Moreover, bAP-NLGN1 was accumulated in the different synapse types formed on proximal and distal dendrites of CA3 pyramidal neurons (<a href="#fig08">Fig. 8<i>D</i></a>). To further determine whether bAP-NLGN1 was localized at excitatory or inhibitory synapses in this preparation, we made slices from KI mice and performed single cell electroporation of CA3 neurons with the intrabodies Xph20-GFP or GPHN.FingR-GFP together with BirA<sup>ER</sup>, as described (<a href="#r18" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r18">18</a>, <a href="#r25" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r25">25</a>) (<a href="#fig08">Fig. 8 <i>E</i> and <i>F</i></a>). Biotinylated bAP-NLGN1 labeled with SA-AF647 localized to both PSD-95 and gephyrin positive puncta (<a href="#fig08">Fig. 8 <i>G</i> and <i>H</i></a>), confirming the results obtained in dissociated neurons.</div><div class="figure-wrap"><header><div class="label">Fig. 8.</div></header><figure id="fig08" class="graphic"><img src="/cms/10.1073/pnas.2411669122/asset/a9363f83-539d-4a81-bd99-a941229ba398/assets/images/large/pnas.2411669122fig08.jpg" height="1460" width="1699" aria-labelledby="fig08" loading="lazy" /><figcaption>Visualization of bAP-NLGN1 in organotypic hippocampal slices by confocal microscopy and TEM. (<i>A</i>) Molecular strategy to biotinylate bAP-NLGN1 and stain it with fluorescent or nanogold streptavidin conjugates. (<i>B</i> and <i>C</i>) Two-color confocal images of CA3 neurons in organotypic slices from WT or KI mice, infected with BirA+ AAV9 (cyan) at DIV3, then labeled with SA-AF647 (magenta) at DIV14. (<i>D</i>) Higher magnification images of synapses (yellow arrowheads) located on proximal (<i>Top</i>) and distal (<i>Bottom</i>) dendrites. (<i>E</i>–<i>H</i>) Single CA3 pyramidal neurons in organotypic hippocampal slices from KI mice were coelectroporated at DIV7 with BirA<sup>ER</sup>, tdTomato, and either Xph20-GFP or GPHN.FingR-GFP, live labeled with SA-AF647 at DIV14, then fixed and observed in confocal microscopy. (<i>E</i> and <i>F</i>) Images of PSD-95 and gephyrin (green), respectively, together with bAP-NLGN1 (magenta), and tdTomato (blue). Arrowheads show bAP-NLGN1 accumulated at postsynapses. (<i>G</i> and <i>H</i>) Proportion and surface density of PSD-95 or gephyrin puncta containing bAP-NLGN1 (7 fields of view, 5 neurons, 2 independent experiments). Data are presented as mean ± SEM and were compared by unpaired <i>t</i> test (<i>G</i>, ****<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001) or Mann–Whitney test (<i>H</i>, *** <i>P</i> = 0.0006). (<i>I</i>–<i>L</i>) TEM images of synapses in the CA3 region of organotypic slices from WT and KI mice, respectively. Slices were infected with BirA+ AAV9, then labeled at DIV14 with SA-Nanogold, fixed, and silver enhanced. Pre- and postsynapses are highlighted in yellow and blue, respectively. (<i>I</i> and <i>J</i>) Canonical synapses formed by CA3 axons onto distal dendrites, with zooms on the synaptic cleft (Right insets). (<i>K</i> and <i>L</i>) Giant synapses formed between mossy fibers and thorny excrescences on proximal dendrites. Red and blue arrows show synaptic and extrasynaptic bAP-NLGN1, respectively. (<i>M</i>) Subcellular distribution of SA-Nanogold particles in the synaptic cleft and in pre- or postsynaptic compartments. Data represent mean ± SEM and were compared by a Kruskal–Wallis test followed by Dunn’s multiple comparison tests (****<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001). (<i>N</i>) Distribution of SA-Nanogold particles across the synaptic cleft (343 particles, 22 synapses). (<i>O</i>) Number of bAP-NLGN1 nanodomains in the synaptic cleft (n = 40 synapses from 3 experiments). (<i>P</i>) Number of SA-Nanogold particles per synapse versus the length of the synaptic cleft.</figcaption></figure></div></section><section id="sec-1-12"><h3>Nanogold Labeling of bAP-NLGN1 Reveals Nanodomains in the Synaptic cleft.</h3><div role="paragraph">Finally, we visualized the distribution of endogenous bAP-NLGN1 in the synaptic cleft at an even higher spatial resolution than dSTORM using TEM, which also has the advantage of highlighting membrane ultrastructure. To this aim, slices from either WT or KI mice were infected with BirA+ AAV9 at DIV3, incubated live at DIV14 with streptavidin conjugated to a 1.4 nm gold nanoparticle (SA-Nanogold), then fixed, embedded in resin, and cut in ultrathin sections before observation in TEM. In cultures from KI mice, there was a striking accumulation of multiple silver-enhanced SA-Nanogold particles that decorated the synaptic cleft, while this type of signal was absent in WT slices (<a href="#fig08">Fig. 8 <i>I</i>–<i>L</i></a>). Some nonspecific silver aggregates were also visible throughout the tissue, but not in synapses. Approximatively 15% of synapses detected by TEM contained nanoparticles, which may be due to a combination of factors: i) Not all neurons are infected by BirA+; ii) not all synapses contain bAP-NLGN1; and iii) the thin 70-nm sections cut for TEM may miss some nanoparticles that are actually present in a 300-nm wide PSD. Overall, the number of SA-Nanogold particles in the synaptic cleft was on average 8.2 ± 1.0, close to the values found in dSTORM. Finally, nanoparticles were distributed all along the synaptic cleft, but often grouped as several subdomains that strikingly resembled those seen in dSTORM, and whose number was again between 1 and 4 (<a href="#fig08">Fig. 8 <i>M</i>–<i>O</i></a>). The number of SA-Nanogold particles did not correlate strongly with the size of the synaptic cleft (<a href="#fig08">Fig. 8<i>P</i></a>), potentially because SA-Nanogold does not penetrate far or the silver precipitate does not form well in dense tissue such as organotypic slices. Alternative explanations could be that large synapses formed on CA3 neurons do not express much NLGN1 but other synaptic binders such as N-cadherin or C1qL (<a href="#r51" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r51">51</a>, <a href="#r52" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r52">52</a>), or that the actual PSD represents only a subfraction of the synaptic cleft.</div></section></section><section id="sec-2" data-type="discussion"><h2>Discussion</h2><div data-type="flushleft" role="paragraph">Advances in our understanding of synaptic connectivity in the brain require a combination of novel animal models and labeling approaches, giving access to the targeting of endogenous neuronal adhesion proteins with high selectivity and spatial resolution. In this context, we generated and characterized a KI mouse strain in which endogenous NLGN1 was tagged with a small peptide (bAP) allowing for its functional biotinylation by exogenously delivered biotin ligase, followed by highly efficient isolation and visualization with various streptavidin conjugates. We reveal that native NLGN1 binds both PSD-95 and gephyrin at the biochemical level, and is equally enriched at excitatory and inhibitory synapses at the microscopy level. We further highlight that bAP-NLGN1 is organized in small nanodomains in the synaptic cleft, whose number scales with postsynapse size.</div><div role="paragraph">By considering a variety of behavioral parameters (body weight, size, fertility, locomotion, anxiety), and judging from the gross anatomy and cell density in different brain structures, KI mice were comparable to WT counterparts. The density of excitatory and inhibitory synapses quantified in either acute hippocampal slices or hippocampal cultures by immunofluorescence staining of pre- and postsynaptic markers, was similar in WT and KI mice. The synaptic ultrastructure evaluated by TEM as well as the AMPA- and NMDA-receptor-dependent synaptic transmission measured by electrophysiology was also similar in WT and KI animals. The levels of most postsynaptic proteins isolated from hippocampal cultures and revealed by Western blot were comparable between WT and KI genotypes, with the exception of bAP-NLGN1 itself whose level was reduced by 26 ± 3 % with respect to NLGN1 from WT mice, in both total and surface protein samples. For comparison, 10%, 50%, and 60% reductions in the protein levels of HA-NLGN1, bAP-GluA2, and SEP-GluA1 were observed in brain lysates from their respective KI mice (<a href="#r37" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r37">37</a>, <a href="#r41" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r41">41</a>, <a href="#r53" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r53">53</a>), suggesting that the introduction of an external tag in a gene of interest tends to reduce subsequent protein production. Interestingly, the larger the tag, e.g. GFP, bAP, HA (238, 15, and 9 aa, respectively), the stronger the reduction in protein level. Specifically, although the bAP tag is the same in our bAP-NLGN1 KI model and in a recently published bAP-GluA2 KI mouse (<a href="#r41" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r41">41</a>), the addition of 48 amino acids encoding the bAP-TEV and linker sequence on GluA2 (5 kDa) was larger than the overall sequence we used here for bAP-NLGN1 (21 amino acids, 3 kDa), such that the band corresponding to bAP-NLGN1 was barely above that of endogenous NLGN1. The reduction in protein level caused by genomic insertion of the bAP-tag can be explained by a perturbation of the transcription machinery, resulting in reduced mRNA level as observed in the SEP-GluA1 KI (<a href="#r53" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r53">53</a>), or by a lower stability of the mRNA transcript. Nevertheless, the amount of bAP-NLGN1 measured in tissue from P0- to P30-old KI mice progressively reached the levels in WT mice, suggesting a developmental adjustment. In any case, the number and ultrastructure of both excitatory and inhibitory synapses in acute slices or cultures from KI mice were identical to those from WT controls, in line with the observation that synapses are generally unaffected in constitutive NLGN1 KO mice (<a href="#r42" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r42">42</a>). There was no change in NLGN2 and a modest increase in NLGN3 protein level in the bAP-NLGN1 KI mouse, indicating little compensation by other NLGN isoforms, in comparison to the constitutive NLGN1 KO which displays increased NLGN3 levels (<a href="#r54" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r54">54</a>) or to the SEP-GluA1 KI which exhibits increases in GluA2/GluA3 expression (<a href="#r53" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r53">53</a>).</div><div role="paragraph">Based on hippocampal cultures from KI mice combined with cell-specific biotinylation through the expression of BirA<sup>ER</sup>, we isolated and labeled bAP-NLGN1 with high selectivity using streptavidin conjugates. Indeed, no NLGN1 was pulled down by streptavidin beads or fluorescently labeled by SA-AF647 in cultures from WT mice expressing BirA<sup>ER</sup>, or in cultures from KI mice not expressing BirA<sup>ER</sup>. Moreover, knocking down endogenous NLGN1 with a previously characterized shRNA (<a href="#r25" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r25">25</a>, <a href="#r31" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r31">31</a>) drastically decreased the surface SA-AF647 signal on neurons from KI mice coexpressing BirA<sup>ER</sup>, demonstrating that streptavidin specifically labels native bAP-NLGN1 and further validating this shRNA to NLGN1. Importantly, biotinylated bAP-NLGN1 retained its adhesive function since it bound NRXN1β expressed in COS-7 cells or neurons. We managed earlier to live-label endogenous NLGN1 with purified NRXN1β-Fc (<a href="#r10" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r10">10</a>, <a href="#r25" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r25">25</a>), but the staining was weak compared to the streptavidin labeling obtained here, likely due to a tremendous difference in binding affinity. Here, streptavidin pull-down of bAP-NLGN1 was highly efficient as it captured roughly 80% of NLGN1 proteins from cell lysates, a level never reached with antibodies to NLGN1 (<a href="#r6" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r6">6</a>, <a href="#r13" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r13">13</a>). Interestingly, whereas NRXN1β-Fc and MDGA1/2-Fc were used to pull-down protein binding partners (<a href="#r55" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r55">55</a>, <a href="#r56" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r56">56</a>), to our knowledge no one ever used NLGN1-Fc as a bait, potentially because of the poor stability of this purified protein (<a href="#r57" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r57">57</a>). Streptavidin pull-down of bAP-NLGN1 allowed the identification of already reported binding partners, including PSD-95 which can interact with the C-terminal PDZ domain binding motif of NLGN1 (<a href="#r8" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r8">8</a>) and NLGN3 which is likely to form heterodimers with NLGN1 through an extracellular coiled coil interface (<a href="#r4" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r4">4</a>, <a href="#r5" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r5">5</a>, <a href="#r58" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r58">58</a>). bAP-NLGN1 also efficiently pulled down NLGN2 and gephyrin, which might occur either through the formation of NLGN1-NLGN2 heterodimers, or through a direct interaction between NLGN1 and the multimeric protein gephyrin, which itself binds NLGN2 (<a href="#r12" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r12">12</a>, <a href="#r29" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r29">29</a>). Yeast two-hybrid and peptide pull-down assays previously showed significant binding between the gephyrin E domain and NLGN1 in vitro (<a href="#r12" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r12">12</a>, <a href="#r13" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r13">13</a>), but this interaction had never been demonstrated in neurons. The coupling between endogenous bAP-NLGN1 and gephyrin likely involves the highly conserved motif (PPDYTLAMRRSPDDIP) located in the middle of the NLGN1 intracellular domain, whose binding to gephyrin is inhibited by tyrosine phosphorylation (<a href="#r13" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r13">13</a>, <a href="#r18" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r18">18</a>). Based on the expression of NLGN1 tyrosine point mutants (Y782A/F) that mimic phosphorylated or nonphosphorylated states, we originally proposed that NLGN1 could be localized at either excitatory or inhibitory synapses, depending on the NLGN1 tyrosine phosphorylation level (<a href="#r13" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r13">13</a>, <a href="#r18" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r18">18</a>), but the actual localization might have been biased by overexpression. Here, we clearly demonstrate that native bAP-NLGN1 is similarly enriched at both excitatory and inhibitory synapses, challenging the belief that NLGN1 is primarily localized at excitatory synapses (<a href="#r28" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r28">28</a>). Similarly, serine phosphorylation can affect NLGN3 function and localization at excitatory vs inhibitory synapses (<a href="#r20" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r20">20</a>, <a href="#r21" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r21">21</a>). Apart from phosphorylation mechanisms, it is possible that different splice variants of NLGN1 are present at excitatory and inhibitory synapses where they would interact with specific NRXNs, e.g., NLGN1 with site B being preferentially targeted to excitatory synapses versus NLGN1 w/o site B at inhibitory synapses, although there are modest amounts of mRNA coding for NLGN1(B-) in rodent brain tissue (<a href="#r33" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r33">33</a>–<a href="#r35" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r35">35</a>). Interestingly, by improving the immunoreactivity of antibodies to synaptic proteins using an alternative fixative to paraformaldehyde (glyoxal), a recent study showed equivalent immunofluorescence signals of endogenous NLGN1 at both excitatory and inhibitory synapses in brain slices (<a href="#r59" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r59">59</a>), while another report demonstrated that roughly 30% of recombinant HA-NLGN1 expressed in primary neurons from conditional NLGN1-4 knock-out mice is localized at gephyrin puncta (<a href="#r36" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r36">36</a>).</div><div role="paragraph">The cell-specific biotinylation procedure allowed us to image bAP-NLGN1 at the cell surface by superresolution microscopy (dSTORM) after live staining with SA-AF647. First, there was a significant reservoir of nonsynaptic bAP-NLGN1 molecules, which might correspond to the diffusive bAP-NLGN1 molecules previously identified by single molecule tracking on moderately overexpressed bAP-NLGN1 (<a href="#r6" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r6">6</a>, <a href="#r25" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r25">25</a>). Second, endogenous bAP-NLGN1 molecules were enriched approximately 3-fold in both PSD-95 and gephyrin puncta as compared to the dendrite shaft. Third, within PSD-95 rich puncta, bAP-NLGN1 formed a subset of nanodomains of average size 50 nm and containing very high protein density. The number (but not the area) of bAP-NLGN1 nanodomains was positively correlated with the surface area of the postsynapse, as reported in brain slices from HA-NLGN1 KI mice immunostained with anti-HA antibodies and processed for expansion microscopy (<a href="#r38" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r38">38</a>). Given that streptavidin is tetravalent and was used at a concentration that saturates biotin binding sites on the neuron surface, one SA-AF647 molecule likely binds two bAP-NLGN1 dimers. Thus, based on the number of streptavidin molecules counted in dSTORM and TEM, we estimate around 16 to 20 bAP-NLGN1 dimers per synapse. Since surface bAP-NLGN1 levels are 26% lower than those of endogenous NLGN1, the actual number of synaptic NLGN1 dimers is likely to be in the range of 22 to 27, close to the 21 copies of NLGN3 molecules estimated by quantitative mass spectrometry (<a href="#r60" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r60">60</a>). Those numbers are five and sixfold larger than the lower estimate of 4 presynaptic NRXN1 molecules quantified by dSTORM on cultures from HA-tagged NRXN1 KI mice (<a href="#r61" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r61">61</a>), but the other NRXN2/3 isoforms (<a href="#r62" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r62">62</a>) may also contribute to bind the many NRXN synaptic partners including NLGNs, LRRTMs, neurexophilins, and cerebellins (<a href="#r63" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r63">63</a>).</div><div role="paragraph">Interestingly, bAP-NLGN1 also formed nanodomains at inhibitory postsynapses, with a similar correlation between the number of domains and the area of gephyrin puncta. Although we did not resolve them here, the scaffolding molecules PSD-95 and gephyrin themselves are known to form substructures with high protein density in the postsynapse, but tend to form larger clusters than bAP-NLGN1 nanodomains (<a href="#r64" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r64">64</a>–<a href="#r67" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r67">67</a>). This observation suggests that bAP-NLGN1 obeys an internal organization which is dictated not only by interactions with the postsynaptic scaffold, but also potentially via interactions with presynaptic NRXNs, which themselves form nanodomains of similar number and size (<a href="#r25" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r25">25</a>, <a href="#r61" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r61">61</a>, <a href="#r62" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r62">62</a>, <a href="#r68" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r68">68</a>). Indeed, 2-color superresolution imaging showed that bAP-NLGN1 nanodomains were in proximity to GFP-NRXN1β and RIM1/2 clusters, likely forming trans-synaptic nanocolumns (<a href="#r48" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r48">48</a>, <a href="#r49" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r49">49</a>). bAP-NLGN1 nanodomains did not occupy the center of synapses as LRRTM2 (<a href="#r25" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r25">25</a>, <a href="#r69" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r69">69</a>, <a href="#r70" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r70">70</a>), or their periphery as N-cadherin or SynCAM1 (<a href="#r71" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r71">71</a>, <a href="#r72" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r72">72</a>), but were rather uniformly spread along the synaptic cleft, where they might interact with either NRXN1 and NRXN3 which occupy different subdomains in the presynaptic membrane (<a href="#r62" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r62">62</a>). Recently, NLGN1 was shown to partition in high-order liquid condensates together with PSD-95 and the AMPA receptor auxiliary subunit stargazin (<a href="#r73" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r73">73</a>, <a href="#r74" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r74">74</a>). This process might also underlie the organization of bAP-NLGN1 in nanodomains seen both at the dSTORM and TEM levels. Likewise, it was shown that gephyrin forms condensates with the cytoplasmic loops of glycine or GABA<sub>A</sub> receptors (<a href="#r74" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r74">74</a>). Given the similarity of organization of bAP-NLGN1 at both excitatory and inhibitory synapses, an interesting perspective would be to evaluate whether gephyrin also phase separates with binding motifs of the NLGN1 intracellular domain (<a href="#r13" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r13">13</a>, <a href="#r75" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r75">75</a>).</div><div role="paragraph">As a conclusion, this tagged KI mouse strain offers the possibility to biochemically isolate endogenous NLGN1 with high yield and thus potentially identify unknown binding partners by proteomics, and to image NLGN1 with great selectivity in single neurons at the nanoscale. Given that it has been notoriously difficult to generate efficient antibodies to many neuronal cell adhesion molecules such as NRXNs and NLGNs, it might be appealing in the future to combine several KI approaches with orthogonal labeling strategies to visualize trans-synaptic complexes in multicolor imaging. Moreover, the expression of biotin ligase with cell-specific promoters might allow the labeling of endogenous bAP-NLGN1 in various cell types, including astrocytes that also express NLGNs (<a href="#r39" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="r39">39</a>). Finally, this KI mouse model might be used as a standard to match the expression level of mutant NLGN1 proteins in rescue conditions to that of native NLGN1, and definitely rule out overexpression artifacts.</div></section><section id="sec-3" data-type="materials methods"><h2>Materials and Methods</h2><div data-type="flushleft" role="paragraph">We generated a KI mouse in which endogenous NLGN1 was fused to the bAP tag. First, we characterized KI mice in comparison with WT counterparts, involving i) behavioral tests; ii) perfusion of mice at P14 and P30, brain sectioning, Nissl staining, immunohistochemistry for VGluT1 and VGAT, and confocal microscopy; iii) staining with osmium, resin embedding, ultrathin sectioning, and TEM; iv) culture of dissociated neurons followed by immunofluorescence staining of synapsin1, VGluT1, VGAT, PSD-95, and gephyrin; v) protein extraction from hippocampal tissue or cultures followed by SDS-PAGE and Western blots; and vi) acute slice preparation and patch clamp recordings in CA1 neurons. Second, we exploited KI mice to isolate or localize biotinylated endogenous bAP-NLGN1 using high affinity streptavidin conjugates, involving i) delivery of biotin ligase (BirA<sup>ER</sup>) using electroporation or adeno-associated viruses (AAVs) in dissociated cultures or organotypic hippocampal slices from KI or WT mice; ii) pull-down of bAP-NLGN1 and protein partners from cell lysates using streptavidin beads, followed by Western blots; iii) visualization of bAP-NLGN1 labeled by fluorescent streptavidin using conventional and superresolution microscopy, in relation to either intrabodies to PSD-95 or gephyrin, presynaptic markers (VGluT1, VGAT, or RIM), or GFP-NRXN1β expressed in COS-7 cells or axons; iv) staining of bAP-NLGN1 with streptavidin-Nanogold followed by silver-enhancement and TEM. Detailed methods are provided as <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials"><i>SI Appendix</i></a>.</div></section></div></section><section id="backmatter" data-extent="backmatter"><div class="core-container"><section id="data-availability" class="core-data-availability"><h2>Data, Materials, and Software Availability</h2><div data-type="flushleft" role="paragraph">All study data are included in the article and/or <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/doi/10.1073/pnas.2411669122#supplementary-materials"><i>SI Appendix</i></a>.</div></section><section id="acknowledgments" role="doc-acknowledgments"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><div role="paragraph">We thank D.    <li class="list-inline-item"><a href="/author/McCarthy%2C+Margaret+M" title="Margaret M. McCarthy">Margaret M. McCarthy</a>&nbsp;and</li>
</ul>
</div><div id="abs-section-id-260fdf8255c2f" class="card__extra-info collapse"><span class="card__extra-info__relation">This is an addendum to</span><a href="#" class="card__extra-info__link">Complex viscosity of helical and doubly helical polymeric liquids from general rigid bead-rod theory</a></div></div></div></li><li><div class="card  "><div class="card-content"><div class="card-header card__header mb-2"><div class="d-flex align-items-center mb-2"><span class="overline card__meta text-uppercase"><a href="/topic/type/research-article" class="card__meta__type">Research Article</a><span class="pl-2 ml-2 pl-2 border-left border-darker-gray  card__meta__date">October 31, 2025</span><span title="Open Access" data-toggle="tooltip" data-placement="bottom" class="pl-2 ml-2 border-left border-darker-gray  card__meta__access meta__access meta__access--open"><i class="icon-open-access"></i></span></span></div><h3 class="article-title card__title"><a href="/doi/10.1073/pnas.2517530122" title="Why more social interactions lead to more polarization in societies" class="text-reset animation-underline">Why more social interactions lead to more polarization in societies</a></h3></div><div class="card-body card__abstract"><div class="card-body card__abstract">In an increasingly interconnected world, rising political polarization threatens social
   cohesion and democratic stability. Here, we demonstrate that the two trends might
   be fundamentally connected: Increasing social connectivity, often seen as ...Over the past two decades, the number of close social connections increased substantially,
   at least by a factor of two. At the same time, societal opinions have become increasingly
   polarized in many Western countries. To explore whether these trends could ...</div></div><div data-visible-items-sm="2" data-visible-items-md="2" data-visible-items="2" data-truncate-less="less" data-truncate-dots="true" class="card-contribs authors text-reset authors-truncate"><ul class="list-inline" title="list of authors">
   <li class="list-inline-item"><a href="/author/Thurner%2C+Stefan" title="Stefan Thurner">Stefan Thurner</a>,</li>

</div><div id="abs-section-id-005975cfef75" class="card__extra-info collapse"><span class="card__extra-info__relation">This is an addendum to</span><a href="#" class="card__extra-info__link">Complex viscosity of helical and doubly helical polymeric liquids from general rigid bead-rod theory</a></div></div></div></li><li><div class="card  "><div class="card-content"><div class="card-header card__header mb-2"><div class="d-flex align-items-center mb-2"><span class="overline card__meta text-uppercase"><a href="/topic/type/research-article" class="card__meta__type">Research Article</a><span class="pl-2 ml-2 pl-2 border-left border-darker-gray  card__meta__date">November 25, 2025</span><span title="Open Access" data-toggle="tooltip" data-placement="bottom" class="pl-2 ml-2 border-left border-darker-gray  card__meta__access meta__access meta__access--open"><i class="icon-open-access"></i></span></span></div><h3 class="article-title card__title"><a href="/doi/10.1073/pnas.2510392122" title="Tattoo ink induces inflammation in the draining lymph node and alters the immune response to vaccination" class="text-reset animation-underline">Tattoo ink induces inflammation in the draining lymph node and alters the immune response to vaccination</a></h3></div><div class="card-body card__abstract"><div class="card-body card__abstract">In this study, we characterized the immune responses to the tattoo ink accumulating
   in the lymph nodes (LNs). This is very relevant as tattoo ink commonly reaches and
   persists in this organ in most tattooed subjects, often lifelong. We have ...Despite safety concerns regarding the toxicity of tattoo ink, no studies have reported
   the consequences of tattooing on the immune response. In this work, we have characterized
   the transport and accumulation of different tattoo inks in the lymphatic ...</div></div><div data-visible-items-sm="2" data-visible-items-md="2" data-visible-items="2" data-truncate-less="less" data-truncate-dots="true" class="card-contribs authors text-reset authors-truncate"><ul class="list-inline" title="list of authors">

        <span role="button" class="password-eye-icon icon-eye apply-btn hidden" aria-label="Show password" tabindex="0" onclick="togglePassword(this)" onkeydown="handleKey(event,this)"></span>
        
                            
    </div>
    <script>
        function togglePassword(el) {
            const passwordInput = document.getElementById("password");
            const isHidden = passwordInput.type === "password";

            // Toggle password visibility
            passwordInput.type = isHidden ? "text" : "password";

            // Safely update the aria-label and classes
            if (el) {
                if (isHidden) {
                    el.setAttribute("aria-label", "Hide password");
                } else {
                    el.setAttribute("aria-label", "Show password");
                }
            }
        }

        function handleKey(event, el) {
            if (event.key === "Enter" || event.key === " ") {
                event.preventDefault();
                togglePassword(el);
            }
        }
    </script>

                        </div>
